data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20layered%20oxide">sodium layered oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode%20material">cathode material</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anionic+Redox&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anionic%20redox">anionic redox</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In recent years, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as competing candidates for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in large-scale applications due to the abundant and globally distributed sodium sources. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Intensive efforts have been devoted to the development of high-performance cathode materials to promote the practical applications of SIBs. Among all the cathode materials, sodium layered transition metal oxides, denoted as Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (TM = Mn, Ni, Co, Fe, etc., 0&lt; <i>x</i> ≤ 1), are regarded the promising candidates due to their facile fabrication process and high energy density. The most investigated types of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> are O3- and P2-type host materials, where O and P represent the octahedral and prismatic location environments, respectively, of sodium ions, and the number stands for the number of TMO<sub>2</sub> layers in each repeated unit cell in the structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> P2-type materials exhibit better rate performance and cycling stability compared to its O3-type counterparts due to the facile Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathway and less complicated phase structure evolution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">However, structure rearrangements such as P2–O2/<i>Z</i> are often reported in P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials due to the sliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> layers, followed by the formation of O-type stacking phase when the content of Na<sup>+</sup> is low in the host materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 5 to 8">(5−8)</a> It is generally accepted that the structure rearrangement between O-type and P-type stacking during the sodiation/desodiation process may lead to several demerits: (1) large volumetric strain due to the shrinkage or expansion along the <i>c-</i>axis, which results in structure instability; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref9" aria-label="reference 7 and 9">(7,9)</a> (2) the huge strain at the phase boundary which initiates the growth of intragranular cracking; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> and (3) negative impact on Na ion diffusion rate due to higher energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in O-type host materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref11" aria-label="reference 4 and 11">(4,11)</a> The phase transition has restrained further enhancement of the cyclability in P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials. In this context, doping/substitution has been developed as an effective strategy to overcome the demerits caused by the structure rearrangement in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials. Dopants/substituents such as Li, Mg, Cu, and Ti have been explored to mitigate the structure rearrangement and enhance the electrochemical performance of P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 4 and 12 to 16">(4,12−16)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Yang and co-workers first reported that Zn substitution can enhance the electrochemical stability in P2-type Na<sub>0.66</sub>Ni<sub>0.33–<i>x</i></sub>Zn<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> After that, there are increasing reports on Zn-containing Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials, such as P2-type Na2/3Mn7/9Zn2/9O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> P2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>(Ni<sub>0.5</sub>Zn<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>0.3</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> and P2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>Zn<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> However, two issues are worth noting in Zn-containing materials. On the one hand, the increase of Zn content will inevitably decrease the reversible capacity based on the cationic redox reaction due to the electrochemical inert properties of Zn. An optimized Zn content is required to achieve a balance between the reversible capacity and cyclability. On the other hand, a new type of phase transition is observed in the abovementioned Zn-containing materials. It seems that the increased amount of Zn content tends to switch the solid solution in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> to a biphase transition in P2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Zn<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, during which large quantities of O-type stacking faults are expected to be introduced to the original P2-type host materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref20" aria-label="reference 9 and 20">(9,20)</a> Up to date, there are rare reports on how to suppress the biphase transition in Zn-containing Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Among all the dopants/substituents, we assume that electrochemically active Cu can be a promising candidate to substitute the electrochemically inert Zn by increasing the reversible capacity based on the cationic redox reaction via participating in charge compensation. The redox reaction of Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> has been reported in many Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 21 to 23">(21−23)</a> In addition, it is also reported that copper can significantly suppress the P2–O2 phase transition in P2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials and improve its cyclability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> In this work, the effect of Cu substitution was investigated in P2-type Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials. The coexistence of Cu and Zn in Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.075</sub>Cu<sub>0.075</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMZC) enables an excellent capacity retention of 96.4% over 150 cycles at 0.1 C rate. The substituted Cu can not only decrease the volume change but also inhibit the anionic redox reaction via participating in charge compensation during the sodiation/desodiation process, therefore suppressing the electrolyte decomposition on the electrode/electrolyte interphase and enhancing the electrochemical performance.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The ratio of metals in the synthesized composites was determined by inductively coupled plasma-optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) tests. The results are provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. It can be seen that the final composites have high consistence with the target stoichiometry. The morphology of the as-prepared composites was investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, the particles in the three samples are irregular microflakes with smooth surface in the range of 1 to several micrometers. No significant difference can be observed among the samples with different contents of Zn and Cu. Their XRD patterns and the corresponding Rietveld refinement results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1d,e</a>. All the diffraction peaks can be indexed to P2-type phase with P63/mmc space group. The lattice parameters <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> in the Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMZ) sample are 2.833 and 11.215 Å, respectively, and vary slightly after partial or complete substitution of Zn with Cu, which is most likely due to the similar ionic radius of Zn<sup>2+</sup> (0.74 Å) and Cu<sup>2+</sup> (0.73 Å). The lattice fringes of the NMZC material are obtained by high-resolution TEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c), and the detected interplanar distance is 0.25 nm, which corresponds well with the calculated distance of (102) planes. The EDS mapping reveals that Na, Ni, Zn, and Mn elements are distributed homogeneously throughout the micron-sized particle of NMZC (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/medium/am2c05521_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns and the calculated XRD patterns by the Rietveld refinement method for NMZ (Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15</sub>O<sub>2</sub>), NMZC, and NMC (Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Cu<sub>0.15</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) samples; (b) TEM and (c) high-resolution TEM images of the NMZC composite; (d) EDS mapping of NMZC micron-sized particles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c05521&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The long-term cycling performance of Na/Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> cells was investigated in the voltage range of 2.2–4.3 V at 30 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). An initial discharge capacity of 112.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was delivered by the NMZ electrode at 0.1 C rate. The copper-containing electrodes show similar initial discharge capacities, with 113.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for NMZC and 116.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Cu<sub>0.15</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMC), respectively. After 150 cycles, only less than 80% of the initial capacity is maintained for NMZ. After the substitution of half Zn with Cu, the capacity retention of NMZC can be significantly improved to 96.4%. Our material demonstrates excellent cycling performance among the recently reported Zn-containing Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials, as can be seen from <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>. The change of capacity retention rate with cycling number is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> to provide a distinct comparison. It can be seen clearly that a further increase of Zn content deteriorates the cycling stability. The Zn-free NMC delivers a capacity of less than 80 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles, which is even worse than NMZ without substitution. This result indicates that both electrochemically active Cu and inert element Zn play vital roles in maintaining good cycling performance in Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials. The charge/discharge profiles and dQ/dV curves in different cycles are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b–e. It can be seen that the substitution of Zn with Cu has a significant impact on the Na sodiation/desodiation behavior as well as its reversibility in the high voltage range. The redox peaks below 2.5 V can be ascribed to the Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction in these materials which occurs reversibly after the initial charge process, and no significant variation can be observed among these materials. The redox peaks in the voltage range of 2.5–3.8 V can be correlated with the Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction and Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Cu<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction, while the sharp peak above 3.8 V can be associated with the Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> or anionic redox reaction in these materials, which usually occur along with the formation of O-type stacking phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> The copper substitution mainly influences the redox behavior in the high voltage range of 3.8–4.3 V. The dQ/dV curves show that the Na/NMZC cell displays a slight degradation in the whole voltage range, which explains the best capacity retention rate among the three cells. By contrast, the sharp redox peak of the Na/NMC cell displays continuous decay between 4.0 and 4.3 V, which is responsible for the unsatisfactory cycling performance. In addition, the rate performance of the three composites is compared at various current rates, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f. NMZC demonstrates good rate capability, with around 50% of the capacity at 0.1 C rate reserved even at the high current rate of 20 C. The rate performance of NMZC is also better than those of NMZ and NMC. It suggests that the coexistence of Zn and Cu is beneficial for cycling performance as well as rate performance.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/medium/am2c05521_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Cycling performance of Na/NMZ, Na/NMZC, and Na/NMC cells at 0.1 C rate over 150 cycles in the voltage range of 2.2–4.3 V. (b) Corresponding charge/discharge curves in the first cycle and (c–e) comparison of d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots in different cycles of Na/NMZ, Na/NMZC, and Na/NMC cells. (f) Rate performance of the cells.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c05521&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In situ XRD tests were carried out to investigate the structural evolution during the charge/discharge process in Na/Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> cells. The in situ XRD test setup is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. The in situ XRD contour maps of NMZ and NMZC electrodes and the corresponding charge/discharge curves are given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>, and the results of the NMC electrode are provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a>. It can be seen clearly that the NMZ electrode experiences a biphase transition upon the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion process in the high voltage range. After an extraction of 0.25 mol Na<sup>+</sup> ions from NMZ at around 4.1 V, the initial (002)<sub>P2</sub> reflection located at around 15.6° disappears and a new (002) diffraction starts to emerge at a higher angle. Considering the similar crystallographic feature of the highly desodiated “<i>Z</i>” phase in Fe-Mn-based Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, the new phase in the Zn-containing material is hereafter referred to as the <i>Z</i> phase for simplicity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref28" aria-label="reference 6, 7 and 28">(6,7,28)</a> With further extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, the (002)<sub><i>Z</i></sub> diffraction peak shifts continuously toward a higher 2θ angle till 16.6° at the end of the charge process, corresponding to the large contraction of the crystalline structure along the <i>c</i>-axis. By contrast, when the Na/NMZ cell is discharged, the (002)<sub><i>Z</i></sub> peak shifts toward a lower angle consecutively till the P2 phase is recovered around 3.8 V. After that, the (002)<sub>P2</sub> reflection shifts slightly to a higher angle upon further discharge. It is worth noting that most of the diffraction peaks with <i>h</i>,<i>k</i> ≠ 0 are smeared into diffuse intensity lines and the substantial broadening of (001) and (101) peaks reflects the loss of long-range order along the <i>c</i>-axis in the regime of <i>Z</i> phase, indicating the low crystallization of the new phase. According to the reports, the evolved <i>Z</i> phase is most likely to compose a large quantity of octahedral stacking Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies which are formed due to the gliding of transition-metal layers after the extraction of a large number of Na<sup>+</sup> ions from the original P2 phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 7, 18 and 19">(7,18,19)</a> By contrast, partial substitution or complete substitution of Zn with Cu can significantly suppress the phase transformation. No diffraction peaks beyond the P2 phase is detected in NMZC and NMC, indicating good preservation of the initial P2 structure in the working voltage range. During the charge process, the (001) diffraction peaks gradually shift toward lower angles, while the (100) and (102) diffraction peaks shift toward higher angles in both Cu-containing electrodes. The shift tendency for these peaks are opposite during the discharge process, indicating reversible structure change during the desodiation/sodiation process.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/medium/am2c05521_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. In situ XRD contour maps and the corresponding charge/discharge profiles for (a) Na/NMZ and (b) Na/NMZC cells.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c05521&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The precise changes of lattice parameters <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> and the unit cell volume are obtained by sequential Rietveld refinement of the in situ XRD patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4b, and S5</a>). During the charge process of NMZ, the lattice parameter <i>c</i> of the P2 structure increases gradually and then decreases significantly in the regime of the <i>Z</i> phase. The increase of the lattice parameter <i>c</i> in the initial charge process agrees well with the interlayer expansion due to the increased repulsion between transition-metal layers after the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, while the subsequent decrease of the lattice parameter <i>c</i> is because of the contraction of sodium layers after the gliding of transition-metal layers. The biphase transformation results in a “diving” phenomenon in parameter <i>c</i> from a peak value of 11.34–10.66 Å and an interruption of the continuous change of the lattice parameter <i>a</i> above 4.0 V in NMZ, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5a</a>. The unit cell volume contracts as large as 7.50% during the charge process in NMZ. By contrast, the lattice parameter <i>c</i> increases and lattice parameter <i>a</i> decreases continuously in both NMZC and NMC, which lead to the expansion of unit volume by 0.78% in NMZC and 0.50% in NMC in the charge process. The phase transition and large volume expansion undoubtedly jeopardize the structural stability. However, the inferior cycle life of NMC compared to NMZ also indicates the importance of the inert element Zn in achieving good cycling stability.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/medium/am2c05521_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Variation of the calculated lattice parameter <i>c</i> and the corresponding charge/discharge profiles during the in situ XRD tests of (a) NMZ and (b) NMZC electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c05521&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The charge compensation mechanism of NMZ, NMZC, and NMC electrodes during the charge process was analyzed by XPS tests. The XPS results of electrodes at the pristine state and charged to 3.7 and 4.2 V are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. It can be seen clearly that the peak position of Mn2p spectra demonstrates barely a shift up to 4.3 V in all electrodes, which suggests that Mn is most possibly in tetravalent state and that no further oxidation occurred during the charge process. By contrast, the peak of Ni2p shifts to a higher position in these electrodes, indicating the oxidation of Ni during the charge process. In addition, the binding energy of Cu also shifts to a higher value, indicating that both Ni and Cu contribute to the charge compensation during the desodiation process in the two Cu-containing electrodes. Moreover, the O1s spectra of NMZ, NMZC, and NMC electrodes in different states are fitted and compared in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. The peak located around 529.8 eV in the pristine electrodes can be assigned to the O<sup>2–</sup> anions from the crystalline framework, while the additional peaks below 531 eV can be attributed to surface-deposited species. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 19, 29 and 30">(19,29,30)</a> However, unlike NMC, the O1s peak of NMZ becomes asymmetric compared to that at 3.7 V when the electrode is charged to 4.3 V. An additional peak at 530.5 eV related to O<sup>1–</sup> with a lower negative charge compared to O<sup>2–</sup> anions is introduced to enable good fitting results, which indicates the occurrence of anionic redox reaction in NMZ. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> This agrees well with the reports that Na–O–Zn configurations tend to trigger the anionic redox reaction due to the weak force between the Zn-3d and O-2p orbitals. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> No peroxo-like (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup><i>n</i>−</sup> species can be detected in charged NMZC and NMC electrodes, which indicates that the substitution of Zn with Cu leads to decreased or suppressed anionic redox reaction in the desodiation process.</div><div class="NLM_p">The influence of Cu substitution on the oxygen redox reaction was further supported by first-principles calculations. The theoretical oxidation process in NMZ, NMZC, and NMC during the desodiation process was predicted by density functional theory (DFT) calculation with hybrid functional (HSE). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> We prepared simplified structural models of Na<sub>0.5</sub>(Mn<sub>0.75</sub>)<sup>4+</sup>(Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Zn<sub>0.125</sub>)<sup>2+</sup>O<sub>2</sub> for NMZ, Na<sub>0.5</sub>(Mn<sub>0.75</sub>)<sup>4+</sup>(Ni<sub>0.125</sub> Zn<sub>0.0625</sub>Cu<sub>0.0625</sub>)<sup>2+</sup>O<sub>2</sub> for NMZC, and Na<sub>0.5</sub>(Mn<sub>0.75</sub>)<sup>4+</sup>(Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Cu<sub>0.125</sub>)<sup>2+</sup>O<sub>2</sub> for NMC. The density of states (DOS) of the three composites with different Na amounts (0.5, 0.375, 0.5, 0.125, and 0) were obtained from DFT calculations for the prediction of their theoretical reaction mechanism, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8a–e</a>. According to the DOS results, the electron densities of Mn 3d orbitals near the Fermi level (<i>E</i><sub>F</sub>) remain zero, and the electron densities of Ni 3d orbitals decrease obviously after the extraction of 0.125 mol of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, suggesting inactive Mn<sup>4+</sup> ions and the usage of Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup> oxidation reaction during this process in all of these models. After this, the change of orbital density varies in the three models. It can be seen that no significant change in Ni 3d orbitals is observed after further extraction of 0.125 mol of Na<sup>+</sup>, and many holes in O 2p orbitals are formed, as shown in the binding energy range from −0.5 to −1.5 eV in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Zn<sub>0.125</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, implying the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions is predominately compensated by the O<sup>2–/1–</sup> oxidation reaction. The contribution from Ni<sup>3+/4+</sup> oxidation reaction, followed by anionic redox reaction is regarded to be responsible for the charge compensation in the following desodiation process of Na<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Zn<sub>0.125</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Similar charge compensation order is also reported in Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, where Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup> oxidation is followed by anionic redox reaction rather than Ni<sup>3+/4+</sup> oxidation reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19 ref35" aria-label="reference 19 and 35">(19,35)</a> After Cu substitution, holes in O2p orbitals emerge in Na<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Zn<sub>0.0625</sub>Cu<sub>0.0625</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and the electron densities on Cu 3d orbital decreased in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Zn<sub>0.0625</sub>Cu<sub>0.0625</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Cu<sub>0.125</sub>O<sub>2</sub> models when <i>x</i> is decreased from 0.375 to 0.25, as we can see from <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>c. Based on the change of electron densities of both Cu3d and Ni3d orbitals as well as hole densities of O 2p orbitals, it can be concluded that both anionic oxidation reaction and Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> oxidation reaction occur in the extraction process of 0.125 Na<sup>+</sup> from Na<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Zn<sub>0.0625</sub>Cu<sub>0.0625</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, while only cationic redox reaction occurs in the extraction process of 0.125 Na<sup>+</sup> from Na<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Cu<sub>0.125</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/medium/am2c05521_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. DOS of O 2p, Ni 3d, Mn 3d, and Cu 3d orbitals on (a) Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Zn<sub>0.125</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.125, 0.25, 0.375, and 0.5) and (b) Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Cu<sub>0.125</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.125, 0.25, 0.375, and 0.5).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c05521&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Combining the results of DFT calculation and XPS results, we infer that the substitution of inert Zn with electrochemically active Cu can decrease the anionic redox reaction in the Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> composites, which agrees well with our speculation. The charge compensation mechanism in NMZ, NMZC, and NMC during the first charge process is deduced, and the schematic illustrations are provided in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 6</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8f</a>. The copper-free and zinc-free samples are first analyzed and compared for simplicity. It can be seen that the initial charge capacity values are 101.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for NMZ and 107.1 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for NMC, which are equivalent to the extraction of 0.39 mol Na<sup>+</sup> ions from NMZ and 0.41 mol of Na<sup>+</sup> ions from NMC. Around 0.3 mol Na<sup>+</sup> ions is extracted based on the oxidation of Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup> first and then O<sup>2–/1–</sup> in NMZ, while only cationic oxidation of Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> contributes to the deintercalation of 0.3 mol Na<sup>+</sup> ions from NMC. Thus, further electron extraction from Ni is responsible for the extraction of 0.09 mol Na<sup>+</sup> ions from NMZ and 0.11 mol Na<sup>+</sup> ions from NMC, respectively, in the high voltage range. Though no peroxo-like (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup><i>n</i>−</sup> species were detected in charged NMZC electrodes, probably due to the limitation of XPS, the DFT calculation results for NMZC and the comparison of charge compensation mechanism in NMZ and NMC indicate the coexistence of oxidation of Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> and anionic redox reaction after Ni<sup>2+/3+</sup> oxidation. The initial charge capacity is 106.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, and the extraction of 0.41 mol Na<sup>+</sup> ions in NMZC is based on both cationic and anionic oxidation reactions. Altogether, the substitution of Zn with Cu lead to increased capacity based on the oxidation reaction of Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> and decreased capacity contributed by anionic redox reaction.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/medium/am2c05521_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Charge/discharge profiles of (a) Na/NMZ and (b) Na/NMC cells in the first cycle and the schematic illustration of charge compensation mechanism in the charging process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c05521&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To explore whether O<sub>2</sub> is produced due to the anionic oxidation reaction, OEMS tests were conducted to monitor the operando gas generation in Na/NMZ, Na/NMZC, and Na/NMC cells at 0.1 C. No O<sub>2</sub> gas was detected in these cells, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, whereas CO<sub>2</sub> evolution was detected mainly from 3.8 V onward which is assigned to the decomposition of carbonate-based solvents in the electrolyte at a high voltage range (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The released CO<sub>2</sub> reaches a total value around 0.034 mol mol<sup>–1</sup> in NMZ, 0.025 mol mol<sup>–1</sup> in NMZC, and 0.011 mol mol<sup>–1</sup> in NMC, respectively. The total amount of CO<sub>2</sub> is decreased with the increase of Cu content in Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Thus, it can be seen that O<sub>2</sub> is generated in none of these cells, and the substitution of Zn with Cu is favorable for suppressing the side reaction between the electrode and electrolyte. It is reported that oxidative species from the lattice oxygen redox reaction may react with the electrolyte to form CO<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 to 39">(37−39)</a></div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/medium/am2c05521_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. CO<sub>2</sub> evolution of (a) Na/NMZ, (b) Na/NMZC, and (c) Na/NMC cells in the first cycle at 0.1 C rate.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/asset/images/large/am2c05521_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c05521&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have explored the influence of Cu substitution in Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.075, and 0.15) on the electrochemical performance and charge compensation process. Cu substitution mainly affects the electrochemical behavior in the high voltage range above 3.8 V. The coexistence of Zn and Cu promises an optimized capacity retention of 96.4% in NMZC over 150 cycles at 0.1 C. In situ XRD analysis confirms that the substitution of Zn with Cu in Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> can significantly suppress the P2–<i>Z</i> phase transition which occurs in NMZ. Additionally, DFT calculation and XPS results demonstrate that Cu substitution in Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials influences the charge compensation process by decreasing the participation of anionic redox and increasing the Cu<sup>2+/3+</sup> redox reaction in the charge compensation process during the desodiation process. The electrode decomposition is effectively suppressed with the incorporation of Cu, probably due to the alleviated bulk structure change and decreased anionic redox reaction, which contribute together to the improved cyclability. This study provides new insights on the underlying mechanism of Cu substitution in improving the cycling performance of sodium layered oxides, which is of significance in the further development of high-performance cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.7</sub>Ni<sub>0.15</sub>Zn<sub>0.15–<i>x</i></sub>Cu<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.075, and 0.15) composites were synthesized by combustion method. In the preparation process, NaNO<sub>3</sub>, Mn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, Ni(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, and Cu(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> in a fixed ratio (Na:Mn:Ni:Zn:Cu = 0.6:0.7:0.15-<i>x</i>:<i>x</i>) were mixed with 80 mL of distilled water to get a transparent solution. Around extra 5 wt % of NaNO<sub>3</sub> was added, considering the loss during high-temperature calcination. Afterward, 6.5 g of sucrose was added to the mixed solution, and the solution was stirred until the dissolution of sucrose. The obtained solution was then heated at 100 °C till a syrupy mass was obtained, which was first calcined at 350 °C for 0.5 h in air and then grounded in a mortar for 1 h. Finally, the powder was calcined in air at 500 °C for 3 h and 900 °C for 15 h to obtain the final product. The obtained material was stored in a vacuum desiccator to protect it from air.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrodes were prepared by blending the obtained materials, polyvinylidene fluoride and acetylene black, in a mass ratio of 7:1:2, with an appropriate amount of 1-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone. The mixture was mixed sufficiently in a mixing defoamer (THINKY, ARM310) and then smeared on a piece of carbon-coated aluminum foil. After drying in a vacuum oven for 12 h at 110 °C, small disks were cut from the foil for coin cell fabrication. The cells were assembled in a glove box filled with argon (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O ≤ 1 ppm). A piece of electrode foil was assembled as a cathode in a 2025-type coin cell which contains a piece of sodium metal and glass fiber, working as an anode and separator, respectively. The obtained coin cells were tested on a Land battery tester. In this work, 1 C rate is equal to 120 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Material Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology of the three samples were characterized by SEM (Hitachi S-4800). The XRD patterns of the as-prepared materials were tested on an X-ray diffractometer (Brucker D8, Germany) with Cu Kα radiation. In situ XRD tests were carried out on a Bruker D8 discover X-ray diffractometer when the coin cells were cycled at the current of 12 mA g<sup>–1</sup> on a Neware battery tester. The cells for in situ XRD test is the same as the coin cells for electrochemical performance test, except that a hole is drilled and sealed with a piece of Kapton film in the center of the cathode cap, and a carbon paper was used as a current collector. The crystal parameters were obtained by refining the obtained XRD patterns on TOPAS 4.2 software. The TEM, high-resolution TEM, and EDS mapping images of NMZC were obtained on an F20 system. XPS spectra were obtained on a Thermo Fisher Scientific K-Alpha surface analysis system with Al Kα X-ray source (1486.8 eV). The XPS data of O1s spectra were refined by using the software of XPS PEAK 4.1. The FWHM values are constrained in the range between 0.5 and 2.5 eV, while the Gaussian–Lorentzian values are set around 20%. The fitting was optimized until the χ<sup>2</sup> value is lower than 5%. The online electrochemical mass spectrometry (OEMS) experiment was performed on a mass spectrometer (modified 5975C mass-selective detector, Agilent), which was connected to a homemade electrochemical flow cell. A specially designed coin cell was located in the flow cell which enables the galvanostatic test and gas flow in and out of the coin cell. The gas evolution condition was monitored when the cell was charged and discharged.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Density Functional Theory Calculations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Spin-polarized DFT calculations were carried out by Vienna Ab-Initio Simulation Package (VASP) in this work. Crystal structures of P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>Zn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>32</sub>, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>16</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>Zn<sub>1</sub>Cu<sub>1</sub>O<sub>32</sub>, and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>O<sub>32</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 2, 4, 6, and 8) were modeled, and k-points 2x1x1 were employed for all the DFT calculations. Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>Zn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>32</sub> and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>12</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>O<sub>32</sub> were set to the antiferromagnetic states, and for Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>16</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>Zn<sub>1</sub>Cu<sub>1</sub>O<sub>32</sub>, the spin on Cu is excess. The energy cutoff was fixed at 450 eV. The cell and atomic structures of all the models were optimized simultaneously with PBE + U, with the U-J values of 3.9 for Mn, 6.0 for Ni, and 7.5 for Cu, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The convergence criteria for the self-consistent field loop are set less than 1 × 10<sup>–6</sup> eV, while the convergence criteria for the cell and geometry are set lower than 0.02 eV/Å. The density of states of O 2p, Cu 3d, Mn 3d, and Ni 3d orbitals were calculated on the accuracy level of the HSE functional. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a></div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c05521?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c05521</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images for the synthesized materials, schematic illustration, and corresponding results of crystallographic parameters by Rietveld refinement; capacity retention rates of Na/NMZ, Na/NMZC, and Na/NMC cells; photos of the in situ XRD test equipment; change of the lattice parameter <i>a</i> and unit cell volume of NMZ, NMZC, and NMC; DOS of O 2p, Ni 3d, Mn 3d, and Cu 3d orbitals on Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Zn<sub>0.0625</sub>Cu<sub>0.0625</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with different Na amounts and schematic illustration of the charge compensation mechanism of NMZC; in situ XRD contour maps and the calculated lattice parameter <i>c</i> for the NMC electrode; XPS spectra of pristine and cycled electrodes for NMZ, NMZC, and NMC; O1s spectra of pristine and cycled electrodes for NMZ, NMZC, and NMC; and O<sub>2</sub> evolution of Na/NMZ, Na/NMZC, and Na/NMC cells (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf">am2c05521_si_001.pdf (1.08 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c05521" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5884</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c2ee02781j</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D5884%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DV.%2BPalomares%26author%3DP.%2BSerras%26author%3DI.%2BVillaluenga%26author%3DK.%2BB.%2BHueso%26author%3DJ.%2BCarretero-Gonzalez%26author%3DT.%2BRojo%26title%3DNa-Ion%2BBatteries%252C%2BRecent%2BAdvances%2Band%2BPresent%2BChallenges%2Bto%2BBecome%2BLow%2BCost%2BEnergy%2BStorage%2BSystems%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc2ee02781j&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c05521&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc2ee02781j&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5884-5901</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201803436"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Owusu, K.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ X-ray diffraction study of P2-Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Zhonghua; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2001</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">148</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. extn. and insertion of Na in P2-Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 was studied by in situ X-ray diffraction.  All the original Na can be extd. from P2-Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 and it can be reversibly inserted again.  When x &gt; 1/3 in Nax[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2, the compd. remains in the P2 structure.  For x ≈ 1/3, a small amt. of O2-type stacking faults are introduced into the structure.  When x &lt; 1/3, the electrode exists as two coexisting phases.  These are P2-Na1/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 (with some O2-type stacking faults) and [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2.  [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 adopts the O2 structure with stacking faults.  As sodium is reinserted in [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 to make Na1/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 the P2 structure forms again.  The evolution of the lattice parameters of P2-Nax[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 (1/3 &lt; x &lt; 2/3) with x and the lattice consts. of [Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 are also reported.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b01146"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Talaie, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.                 10.1021/acs.chemmater.7b01146</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D29%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D6684%26journal%3DChem.%2BMater.%26author%3DE.%2BTalaie%26author%3DS.%2BY.%2BKim%26author%3DN.%2BChen%26author%3DL.%2BF.%2BNazar%26title%3DStructural%2BEvolution%2Band%2BRedox%2BProcesses%2BInvolved%2Bin%2Bthe%2BElectrochemical%2BCycling%2Bof%2BP2%25E2%2580%2593Na0.67%255BMn0.66Fe0.20Cu0.14%255DO2%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.chemmater.7b01146&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c05521&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.chemmater.7b01146&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structural Evolution and Redox Processes Involved in the Electrochemical Cycling of P2-Na0.67[Mn0.66Fe0.20Cu0.14]O2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Talaie, Elahe; Kim, Se Young; Chen, Ning; Nazar, Linda F.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">29</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">16</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6684-6697</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the synthesis and structural evolution of P2-Na0.67[Mn0.66Fe0.20Cu0.14]O2 during charge and discharge as a pos. electrode for Na-ion batteries.  Operando X-ray diffraction anal. revealed the existence of two phase transitions in the voltage window between 1.5-4.3 V.  Ex situ pair distribution function anal. was used to characterize the local structure of the high-voltage phase which is disordered along the c-axis and is derived by the migration of a fraction of iron ions into the interlayer space.  Operando X-ray absorption spectroscopy was employed to monitor the local structural evolution of the transition metals and shows the existence of Mn3+/4+ redox below ∼3.4 V, followed by the redox activity of Cu and Fe ions at higher voltages.  However, no change was obsd. in the K-edge X-ray absorption near-edge spectrum of any of the transition metals at voltages above 4.1 V, where the growth of the high-voltage phase is initiated.  The combination of these results implies the reversible contribution of oxide ion redox to the capacity, which is coincident with metal migration.  The above processes result in voltage fading over cycling, similar to that exhibited by Li2MnO3-based pos. electrode materials in Li-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C8EE02991A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Somerville, J. G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nature of the “Z”-Phase in Layered Na-Ion Battery Cathodes</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2223</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C8EE02991A</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D12%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D2223%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DJ.%2BW.%2BSomerville%26author%3DA.%2BSobkowiak%26author%3DN.%2BTapia-Ruiz%26author%3DJ.%2BBillaud%26author%3DJ.%2BG.%2BLozano%26author%3DR.%2BA.%2BHouse%26author%3DL.%2BC.%2BGallington%26author%3DT.%2BEricsson%26author%3DL.%2BH%25C3%25A4ggstr%25C3%25B6m%26author%3DM.%2BR.%2BRoberts%26author%3DU.%2BMaitra%26author%3DP.%2BG.%2BBruce%26title%3DNature%2Bof%2Bthe%2B%25E2%2580%259CZ%25E2%2580%259D-Phase%2Bin%2BLayered%2BNa-Ion%2BBattery%2BCathodes%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC8EE02991A&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c05521&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC8EE02991A&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nature of the "Z"-phase in layered Na-ion battery cathodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Somerville, James W.; Sobkowiak, Adam; Tapia-Ruiz, Nuria; Billaud, Juliette; Lozano, Juan G.; House, Robert A.; Gallington, Leighanne C.; Ericsson, Tore; Haggstrom, Lennart; Roberts, Matthew R.; Maitra, Urmimala; Bruce, Peter G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2223-2232</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered sodium transition metal oxides with the P2 structure, e.g.  Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2, are regarded as candidates for Na-ion battery cathodes.  On charging, extn. of Na destabilizes the P2 phase (ABBA oxide ion stacking) in which Na+ is in trigonal prismatic coordination, resulting in layer gliding and formation of an O2 phase (ABAC stacking) with octahedral coordination.  However, many related compds. do not exhibit such a simple P2 to O2 transition but rather form a so called "Z"-phase.  Substituting Ni by Fe in Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 is attractive as it reduces cost.  The Fe contg. compds., such as Na2/3[Ni1/6Mn1/2Fe1/3]O2, form a "Z"-phase when charged above 4.1 V vs.  Na+/Na.  By combining ex situ and operando X-ray diffraction with scanning transmission electron microscopy and simulated diffraction patterns, we demonstrate that the "Z"-phase is most accurately described as a continuously changing intergrowth structure which evolves from P2 to O2 through the OP4 structure as an intermediate.  On charging, Na+ removal results in O-type stacking faults within the P2 structure which increase in proportion.  At 50% O-type stacking faults, the ordered OP4 phase forms and on further charging more O-type stacking faults are formed progressing towards a pure O2 structure.  This gives the superficial appearance of a solid soln.  Furthermore, in contrast to some previous studies, we did not detect Fe migration at any state-of-charge using 57Fe-Mossbauer spectroscopy.  It was, however, found that the Fe-substitution serves to disrupt cation ordering in the material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2020.104548"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Dual Carbon Decorated Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>: A High-Energy-Density Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>70</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">104548</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">NASICON-structured Na3MnTi(PO4)3 (NMTP) is a high-energy sodium-ion battery (SIB) cathode material.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Phase transition induced cracking plaguing layered cathode for sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Kuan; Yan, Pengfei; Sui, Manling</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. charging induced phase transition is a common and thermodynamically-driven phenomenon for variety of cathode materials, which couples with chem. and mech. effects leading to performance degrdn.  Phase transition is particularly complex for layered sodium transition metal oxides and its related detrimental effects remain elusive.  Herein, we take P2-type Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 (P2-NNM) as an example to scrutiny the detrimental consequences upon high voltage cycling.  We find that repeated P2-O2 phase transition breaks down cathode primary grains by generating high d. of intragranular cracks, which is qual. proved to be the main cause of performance decay.  Intriguingly, the nucleation and growth of intragranular crack is through loss of atoms rather than cleavage, resembling the stress corrosion cracking mechanism which preferentially nucleates at P2/O2 phase boundary.  Moreover, we find the P2-structured cathode is not sensitive to surface degrdn., which explains the superior performance of P2-NNM cathode when cycling at low voltage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.scib.2018.02.012"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Senga, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Suenaga, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cation-Mixing Stabilized Layered Oxide Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Sci.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Cation-mixing stabilized layered oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Shaohua; Sun, Yang; Liu, Pan; Yi, Jin; He, Ping; Zhang, Xiaoyu; Zhu, Yanbei; Senga, Ryosuke; Suenaga, Kazu; Chen, Mingwei; Zhou, Haoshen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science Bulletin</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are promising for large-scale energy storage due to sodium's low cost and infinite abundance.  The most popular cathodes for sodium-ion batteries, i.e., the layered sodium-contg. oxides, usually exhibit reversible host rearrangement between P-type and O-type stacking upon charge/discharge.  Herein we demonstrate that such host rearrangement is unfavorable and can be suppressed by introducing transition-metal ions into sodium layers.  The electrode with stabilized P3-type stacking delivers superior rate capability, high energy efficiency, and excellent cycling performance.  Owing to the cation-mixing nature, it performs the lowest lattice strain among all reported cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.  Our findings highlight the significance of a stable host for sodium-ion storage and moreover underline the fundamental distinction in material design strategy between lithium- and sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b18226"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">New Insights into the Roles of Mg in Improving the Rate Capability and Cycling Stability of O3-NaMn<sub>0.48</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Insights into the Roles of Mg in Improving the Rate Capability and Cycling Stability of O3-NaMn0.48Ni0.2Fe0.3Mg0.02O2 for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Cheng; Gao, Rui; Zheng, Lirong; Hao, Yongmei; Liu, Xiangfeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">13</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10819-10827</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Elements doping has been used to improve the electrochem. performances of O3-type layered transition metal oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.  However, their roles and the improvement mechanism have not been clearly understood.  Herein, the effects of Mg substitution for Mn on the structure and electrochem. performances of NaMn0.48Ni0.2Fe0.3Mg0.02O2 have been comprehensively investigated and some new insights into the roles of Mg in improving the rate capability and cycling stability have been presented. (1) The substitution of Mg for Mn enlarges the interlayer spacing, which not only enhances Na+ diffusion and the rate capability but also alleviates the lattice strains induced by Na+ intercalation/deintercalation. (2) The substitution of Mg by Mn also shrinks TM-O bond and TMO2 slabs, which enhances the layered structure stability. (3) The Mg substitution also mitigates the structure distortion or vol. change of the crystal lattices and suppresses the irreversible phase transitions. (4) The substitution of low-valence Mg2+ for Mn3+ reduces Mn3+ and minimizes Jahn-Teller effect, which also further alleviates the irreversible phase transformations and improves the layered structure stability.  This study not only unveils the roles of Mg but also presents some insights into designing the cathode materials with both high rate capability and high cycling stability through the lattice structure regulation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b00614"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, T.-R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Z.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, E.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, X.-D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, B.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cu<sup>2+</sup> Dual-Doped Layer-Tunnel Hybrid Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>1–x</sub>Cu<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as a Cathode of Sodium-Ion Battery with Enhanced Structure Stability, Electrochemical Property, and Air Stability</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been regarded as a promising candidate for large-scale renewable energy storage system.  Layered Mn oxide cathode possesses the advantages of high energy d., low cost and natural abundance while suffering from limited cycling life and poor rate capacity.  To overcome these weaknesses, layer-tunnel hybrid material was developed and served as the cathode of SIB, which integrated high capacity, superior cycle ability, and rate performance.  Here, the doping of Cu was adopted to suppress the Jahn-Teller effect of Mn3+ and to affect relevant structural parameters.  Multifunctions of the Cu2+ doping were studied.  It was found that the structure component ratio is varied with the Cu2+ doping amt.  Results demonstrated that Na+/vacancy rearrangement and phase transitions were suppressed during cycling without sacrificing the reversible capacity and enhanced electrochem. performances evidenced with 96 mAh/g retained after 250 cycles at 4 C and 85 mAh/g at 8 C.  Ex situ x-ray diffraction has demonstrated high reversibility of the Na0.6Mn0.9Cu0.1O2 cathode during Na+ extn./insertion processes and superior air stability that results in better storage properties.  This study reveals that the Cu2+ doping could be an effective strategy to tune the properties and related performances of Mn-based layer-tunnel hybrid cathode.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b16077"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, S.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na+/Vacancy Disordered P2-Na0.67Co1-xTixO2: High-Energy and High-Power Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kang, Seok Mun; Park, Jae-Hyuk; Jin, Aihua; Jung, Young Hwa; Mun, Junyoung; Sung, Yung-Eun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although sodium ion batteries (NIBs) have gained wide interest, their poor energy d. poses a serious challenge for their practical applications.  Therefore, high-energy-d. cathode materials are required for NIBs to enable the utilization of a large amt. of reversible Na ions.  This study presents a P2-type Na0.67Co1-xTixO2 (x &lt; 0.2) cathode with an extended potential range higher than 4.4 V to present a high specific capacity of 166 mAh g-1.  A group of P2-type cathodes contg. various amts. of Ti is prepd. using a facile synthetic method.  These cathodes show different behaviors of the Na+/vacancy ordering.  Na0.67CoO2 suffers severe capacity loss at high voltages due to irreversible structure changes causing serious polarization, while the Ti-substituted cathodes have long credible cycleability as well as high energy.  In particular, Na0.67Co0.90Ti0.10O2 exhibits excellent capacity retention (115 mAh g-1) even after 100 cycles, whereas Na0.67CoO2 exhibits negligible capacity retention (&lt;10 mAh g-1) at 4.5 V cutoff conditions.  Na0.67Co0.90Ti0.10O2 also exhibits outstanding rate capabilities of 108 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 1000 mA g-1 (7.4 C).  Increased sodium diffusion kinetics from mitigated Na+/vacancy ordering, which allows high Na+ utilization, are investigated to find in detail the mechanism of the improvement by combining systematic analyses comprising TEM, in situ XRD, and electrochem. methods.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1002/anie.202108933"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shadike, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.-C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.-N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Stabilizing Transition Metal Vacancy Induced Oxygen Redox by Co<sup>2+</sup>/Co<sup>3+</sup> Redox and Sodium-Site Doping for Layered Cathode Materials</span>. <i>Angew.
