                        cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stabilizing Transition Metal Vacancy Induced Oxygen Redox by Co2+/Co3+ Redox and Sodium-Site Doping for Layered Cathode Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xun-Lu; Bao, Jian; Shadike, Zulipiya; Wang, Qin-Chao; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Zhou, Yong-Ning; Sun, Dalin; Fang, Fang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Anionic redox is an effective way to boost the energy d. of layer-structured metal-oxide cathodes for rechargeable batteries.  However, inherent rigid nature of the TMO6 (TM: transition metals) subunits in the layered materials makes it hardly tolerate the inner strains induced by lattice glide, esp. at high voltage.  Herein, P2-Na0.8Mg0.13[Mn0.6Co0.2Mg0.07.box.0.13]O2 (.box.: TM vacancy) is designed that contains vacancies in TM sites, and Mg ions in both TM and sodium sites.  Vacancies make the rigid TMO6 octahedron become more asym. and flexible.  Low valence Co2+/Co3+ redox couple stabilizes the electronic structure, esp. at the charged state.  Mg2+ in sodium sites can tune the interlayer spacing against O-O electrostatic repulsion.  Time-resolved in situ X-ray diffraction confirms that irreversible structure evolution is effectively suppressed during deep desodiation benefiting from the specific configuration.  X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and d. functional theory (DFT) calcns. demonstrate that, deriving from the intrinsic vacancies, multiple local configurations of ".box.-O-.box.", "Na-O-.box.", "Mg-O-.box." are superior in facilitating the oxygen redox for charge compensation than previously reported "Na-O-Mg".  The resulted material delivers promising cycle stability and rate capability, with a long voltage plateau at 4.2 V contributed by oxygen, and can be well maintained even at high rates.  The strategy will inspire new ideas in designing highly stable cathode materials with reversible anionic redox for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202008194"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.-L.</span></span>;                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Whole-Voltage-Range Oxygen Redox in P2-Layered Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xun-Lu; Wang, Tian; Yuan, Yifei; Yue, Xin-Yang; Wang, Qin-Chao; Wang, Jun-Yang; Zhong, Jun; Lin, Ruo-Qian; Yao, Yuan; Wu, Xiao-Jing; Yu, Xi-Qian; Fu, Zheng-Wen; Xia, Yong-Yao; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Liu, Tongchao; Amine, Khalil; Shadike, Zulipiya; Zhou, Yong-Ning; Lu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">33</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">13</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2008194</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Oxygen-redox of layer-structured metal-oxide cathodes has drawn great attention as an effective approach to break through the bottleneck of their capacity limit.  However, reversible oxygen-redox can only be obtained in the high-voltage region (usually over 3.5 V) in current metal-oxide cathodes.  Here, we realize reversible oxygen-redox in a wide voltage range of 1.5-4.5 V in a P2-layered Na0.7Mg0.2[Fe0.2Mn0.6.box.0.2]O2 cathode material, where intrinsic vacancies are located in transition-metal (TM) sites and Mg-ions are located in Na sites.  Mg-ions in the Na layer serve as "pillars" to stabilize the layered structure during electrochem. cycling, esp. in the high-voltage region.  Intrinsic vacancies in the TM layer create the local configurations of ".box.-O-.box.", "Na-O-.box." and "Mg-O-.box." to trigger oxygen-redox in the whole voltage range of charge-discharge.  Time-resolved techniques demonstrate that the P2 phase is well maintained in a wide potential window range of 1.5-4.5 V even at 10 C.  It is revealed that charge compensation from Mn- and O-ions contributes to the whole voltage range of 1.5-4.5 V, while the redox of Fe-ions only contributes to the high-voltage region of 3.0-4.5 V.  The orphaned electrons in the nonbonding 2p orbitals of O that point toward TM-vacancy sites are responsible for reversible oxygen-redox, and Mg-ions in Na sites suppress oxygen release effectively.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.12.083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">McDonald, M.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-type Na0.66Ni0.33-xZnxMn0.67O2 as new high-voltage cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Xuehang; Guo, Jianghuai; Wang, Dawei; Zhong, Guiming; McDonald, Matthew J.; Yang, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">281</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">18-26</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type Na0.66Ni0.33-xZnxMn0.67O2 (x = 0, 0.07, 0.14) are prepd. using a conventional solid state method and for the first time developed as promising cathode materials for high-voltage sodium-ion batteries.  The x-ray diffraction patterns show that Zn2+ ions are successfully incorporated into the lattice of the Na-Ni-Mn-O system and the P2-type structure remains unchanged after substitution.  The introduction of Zn2+ in the Na-Ni-Mn-O system can effectively overcome the drawback of voltage decay when charged to a higher cutoff voltage (&gt;4.0 V), and significantly improve capacity retention compared to the unsubstituted material during cycling.  In addn., a smoother charge/discharge profile can be obsd. between 3.0 and 4.0 V for Zn-substituted samples, demonstrating that Na+/vacancy ordering can be suppressed during sodium insertion/extn.  Na0.66Ni0.26Zn0.07Mn0.67O2 can deliver an initial capacity of 132 mA-h/g at 12 mA/g with a high av. voltage of 3.6 V and a capacity retention of 89% after 30 cycles.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy measurements demonstrate that Zn substitution is an effective way to limit the increase of inter-particle contact resistance by suppressing any possible irreversible phase transformation found at low sodium contents.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201802379"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sathiya, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mendoza-Sánchez, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iadecola, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vergnet, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dedryvère, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saubanère, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abakumov, A.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Voltage Oxygen-Redox-Based Cathode for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Konarov, Aishuak; Kim, Hee Jae; Jo, Jae-Hyeon; Voronina, Natalia; Lee, Yongseok; Bakenov, Zhumabay; Kim, Jongsoon; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">24</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2001111</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, anionic-redox-based materials have shown promising electrochem. performance as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries.  However, one of the limiting factors in the development of oxygen-redox-based electrodes is their low operating voltage.  In this study, the operating voltage of oxygen-redox-based electrodes is raised by incorporating nickel into P2-type Na2/3[Zn0.3Mn0.7]O2 in such a way that the zinc is partially substituted by nickel.  As designed, the resulting P2-type Na2/3[(Ni0.5Zn0.5)0.3Mn0.7]O2 electrode exhibits an av. operating voltage of 3.5 V and retains 95% of its initial capacity after 200 cycles in the voltage range of 2.3-4.6 V at 0.1C (26 mA g-1).  Operando X-ray diffraction anal. reveals the reversible phase transition: P2 to OP4 phase on charge and recovery to the P2 phase on discharge.  Moreover, ex situ X-ray absorption near edge structure and XPS studies reveal that the capacity is generated by the combination of Ni2+/Ni4+ and O2-/O1- redox pairs, which is supported by first-principles calcns.  It is thought that this kind of high voltage redox species combined with oxygen redox could be an interesting approach to further increase energy d. of cathode materials for not only sodium-based rechargeable batteries, but other alkali-ion battery systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2019.02.042"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Konarov, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jo, J.                 10.1016/j.nanoen.2019.02.042</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D59%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D197%26journal%3DNano%2BEnergy%26author%3DA.%2BKonarov%26author%3DJ.%2BH.%2BJo%26author%3DJ.%2BU.%2BChoi%26author%3DZ.%2BBakenov%26author%3DH.%2BYashiro%26author%3DJ.%2BKim%26author%3DS.-T.%2BMyung%26title%3DExceptionally%2BHighly%2BStable%2BCycling%2BPerformance%2Band%2BFacile%2BOxygen-Redox%2Bof%2BManganese-Based%2BCathode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BRechargeable%2BSodium%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.nanoen.2019.02.042&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c05521&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.nanoen.2019.02.042&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exceptionally highly stable cycling performance and facile oxygen-redox of manganese-based cathode materials for rechargeable sodium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Konarov, Aishuak; Jo, Jae Hyeon; Choi, Ji Ung; Bakenov, Zhumabay; Yashiro, Hitoshi; Kim, Jongsoon; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">59</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">197-206</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this study, the effect of Zn doping on the electrochem. properties of P2-Na2/3[Mn1-xZnx]O2 (x = 0.0, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3) is investigated for the first time.  The P2-Na2/3[Mn0.7Zn0.3]O2 electrode deliveres a specific discharge capacity of approx. 190 mAh g-1 based on the oxygen-redox reaction (O2-/O1-), after which the Mn4+/Mn3+ redox reaction contributes to the capacity.  The cycling performance of the P2-Na2/3[Mn0.7Zn0.3]O2 electrode is also greatly enhanced compared with that of the P2-Na2/3MnO2 electrode (capacity retention of 80% vs. 30% after 200 cycles).  This improved cyclability is due to the suppression of cooperative Jahn-Teller distortion as well as stabilization of the structure by the electrochem. inactive Zn2+ ions.  First-principle calcns. and exptl. anal., including XPS and X-ray absorption near edge structure spectroscopy, clearly confirms that the Zn2+ substitution in P2-Na2/3MnO2 enables the O2-/O1- redox reaction.  In addn., time-of-flight secondary ion mass spectroscopy anal. reveals that no sodium carbonates forms on the electrode surface.  Our findings provide a potential new path to utilize cost-effective Mn-rich cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries via not only cationic redox but also anodic redox.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1088/1674-1056/23/11/118202"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.-Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Novel Copper Redox-Based Cathode Materials for Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chin.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Novel copper redox-based cathode materials for room-temperature sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Shu-Yin; Wu, Xiao-Yan; Li, Yun-Ming; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Li-Quan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chinese Physics B</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">IOP Publishing Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered oxides of P2-type Na0.68Cu0.34Mn0.66O2, P2-type Na0.68Cu0.34Mn0.50Ti0.16O2, and O'3-type NaCu0.67Sb0.33O2 were synthesized and evaluated as cathode materials for room-temp. sodium-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type sodium layered oxides NaxMO2 (M = transition metal) are considered as one kind of promising cathode material for sodium-ion batteries because of their known structures, superior electrochem. properties, and their ease of synthesis.  The Ni2+/Ni3+ and Ni3+/Ni4+ redox reactions endow the P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 electrode with a relatively high operating voltage and high specific capacity.  However, the phase transition from P2 to O2 and Na+/vacancy ordering make P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 susceptible to severe voltage and capacity decay.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries have garnered significant research attention in recent years due to the rising demand for large-scale energy storage solns. as well as the high abundance of sodium.  P2-type layered oxide materials have been identified as promising pos. electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.  Previously, P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 was shown to have a high operating voltage and high capacity but suffers from a step-like voltage curve and capacity loss during cycling, potentially due to its P2-O2 transition at high voltages.  One strategy to improve cycling performance has been to dope Ni2+ with other 2+ cations, such as Zn2+ or Mg2+, which improved capacity retention but significantly decreases reversible capacity, since these ions were not electrochem. active.  Since Cu2+ has been shown to be electrochem. active, we replaced Ni2+ with Cu2+, resulting in air-stable Na2/3Ni1/3-xCuxMn2/3O2 (0 ≤ x ≤ 1/3).  Both Ni2+/Ni4+ and Cu2+/Cu3+ participate in the redox reaction during cycling, capacity retention was greatly improved, and phase changes were suppressed during cycling without sacrificing much capacity.  The material retains a P2/OP4 structure even when cycled to high voltages.  The doping strategy is a promising approach for the future development of pos. electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1039/D0TA08268F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, J.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, C.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insights into the Li Incorporation Effect in Ni/Co-Free P2-type Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Cu<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Copper-contg. composites have been widely investigated among various sodium-layered cathode materials due to the low cost and abundance of the copper element.  However, the electrochem. performance of these composites is usually deteriorated by the phase transitions corresponding to the stepwise charge/discharge curves, leading to an unsatisfactory capacity stability.  Herein, we report that an improved electrochem. performance was achieved by substituting manganese with lithium in the Ni/Co-free P2-type Na0.6Mn0.8-xLixCu0.2O2-δ (x = 0, 0.05, 0.1, 0.15 and 0.2, δ &gt; 0) composites with smooth charged/discharged curves. The interphase changes during and after cycling were studied by ex situ and in situ electrochem. impedance spectroscopy measurements, which prove that Li benefits the charge transfer and stabilizes the interphase of the Na0.6Mn0.65Li0.15Cu0.2O2-δ electrode.  The in situ X-ray diffractometry results illustrate an alleviated crystallog. change in Na0.6Mn0.65Li0.15Cu0.2O2-δ compared to the Li-free electrode.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Abstr.: Layered transition metal oxides are the most important cathode materials for Li/Na/K ion batteries.  Suppressing undesirable phase transformations during charge-discharge processes is a crit. and fundamental challenge towards the rational design of high-performance layered oxide cathodes.  Here we report a shale-like NaxMnO2 (S-NMO) electrode that is derived from a simple but effective water-mediated strategy.  This strategy expands the Na+ layer spacings of P2-type Na0.67MnO2 and transforms the particles into accordion-like morphol.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A combination of operando X-ray diffraction, pair distribution function (PDF) anal. coupled with electrochem. measurements and M.ovrddot.ossbauer spectroscopy elucidates the nature of the phase transitions induced by insertion and extn. of sodium ions in P2-Na0.67[NiyMn0.5+yFe0.5-2y]O2 (y = 0, 0.10, 0.15).  When phase transitions are avoided, the optimal cathode material - P2-Na0.67Fe0.2Mn0.65Ni0.15O2 - delivers 25% more energy than the unsubstituted material, sustaining high specific energy (350 Wh kg-1) at moderate rates and maintains 80% of the original energy d. after 150 cycles - a significant improvement in performance vs. the unsubstituted analog.  The crystal structure of the high voltage phase is solved for the first time by X-ray PDF anal. of P2-Na0.67-zFe0.5Mn0.5O2 (where z ∼ 0.5), revealing that migration of the transition metals - particularly Fe3+ - into tetrahedral sites in the interlayer space occurs at high potential.  This results in new short range order between two adjacent layers.  Although the transition metal migration is reversible as proven by electrochem. performance, it induces a large disfavorable cell polarization.  The deleterious high voltage transition is mitigated by substitution of Fe3+ by Mn4+/Ni2+, giving rise to better cycling performance.  Moreover, as demonstrated by 57Fe M.ovrddot.ossbauer spectroscopy, the much lower ratio of Fe4+O6 to Fe3+O6 obsd. systematically across the range of Ni content - compared to the values expected from a purely ionic model - suggests redox activity involves the O-2p orbitals owing to their overlap with the transition metal-3d orbitals.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2020.126308"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Butt, F.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">X-ray Photoemission Spectroscopy Study of Cationic and Anionic Redox Processes in High-Capacity Li-Ion Battery Layered-Oxide Electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Foix, Dominique; Sathiya, Mariyappan; McCalla, Eric; Tarascon, Jean-Marie; Gonbeau, Danielle</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrode materials based on Li-rich layered oxides are of growing interest for high-energy Li-ion battery applications because of their staggering capacities assocd. with the emergence of a novel, reversible anionic process.  However, the fundamental science at work behind this new process needs to be well understood for further optimization.  Here we report on the redox mechanisms in high-capacity Li-rich materials Li2Ru1-xMxO3 and Li2Ir1-xMxO3, by combining X-ray photoemission spectroscopy (XPS) core peaks and valence intensity analyses.  We fully confirm that these materials electrochem. react with Li via cumulative reversible cationic/anionic redox processes, but more importantly we reveal that, depending on the nature of the metal (Ru or Ir), there is a delicate balance between metal and oxygen contributions.  For instance, we show a greater implication of oxide ions for Ir-based electrodes, consistent with the higher covalent character of Ir-O bonds compared to Ru-O bonds.  We equally provide evidence that the oxygen redox process is responsible for the high capacity displayed by the Li-rich NMC Li1.2Ni0.13Co0.13Mn0.54O2 electrodes that are serious contenders for the next generation of Li-ion batteries.  These combined results highlight the benefit of collecting both XPS core and valence spectra for a better understanding of anionic redox mechanisms in Li-rich layered oxides.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-017-02291-9"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Assat, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Foix, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delacourt, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iadecola, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dedryvère, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fundamental Interplay between Anionic/Cationic Redox Governing the Kinetics and Thermodynamics of Lithium-Rich Cathodes</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fundamental interplay between anionic/cationic redox governing the kinetics and thermodynamics of lithium-rich cathodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Assat Gaurav; Tarascon Jean-Marie; Assat Gaurav; Foix Dominique; Delacourt Charles; Iadecola Antonella; Dedryvere Remi; Tarascon Jean-Marie; Assat Gaurav; Tarascon Jean-Marie; Foix Dominique; Dedryvere Remi; Delacourt Charles</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Reversible anionic redox has rejuvenated the search for high-capacity lithium-ion battery cathodes.  Real-world success necessitates the holistic mastering of this electrochemistry's kinetics, thermodynamics, and stability.  Here we prove oxygen redox reactivity in the archetypical lithium- and manganese-rich layered cathodes through bulk-sensitive synchrotron-based spectroscopies, and elucidate their complete anionic/cationic charge-compensation mechanism.  Furthermore, via various electroanalytical methods, we answer how the anionic/cationic interplay governs application-wise important issues-namely sluggish kinetics, large hysteresis, and voltage fade-that afflict these promising cathodes despite widespread industrial and academic efforts.  We find that cationic redox is kinetically fast and without hysteresis unlike sluggish anions, which furthermore show different oxidation vs. reduction potentials.  Additionally, more time spent with fully oxidized oxygen promotes voltage fade.  These fundamental insights about anionic redox are indispensable for improving lithium-rich cathodes.  Moreover, our methodology provides guidelines for assessing the merits of existing and future anionic redox-based high-energy cathodes, which are being discovered rapidly.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2021.139421"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Symmetric Sodium-Ion Battery Based on P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>[Zn<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>1-<i>x</i></sub>]O<sub>2</sub> as Both Positive and Negative Electrode Materials</span>. <i>Electrochim.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries have been explored extensively due to its abundant reserve and low cost.  However, reports on full sym. battery with the same electrode materials are relatively less than asym. battery.  In this work, sym. sodium-ion battery based on layered P2-Na0.67[ZnxMn1-x]O2 (x = 0.1, 0.2, 0.28, 0.34) as both pos. and neg. electrode materials are studied comprehensively.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hybrid d. functionals are very successful in describing a wide range of mol. properties accurately.  In large mols. and solids, however, calcg. the exact (Hartree-Fock) exchange is computationally expensive, esp. for systems with metallic characteristics.  In the present work, we develop a new hybrid d. functional based on a screened Coulomb potential for the exchange interaction which circumvents this bottleneck.  The results obtained for structural and thermodn. properties of mols. are comparable in quality to the most widely used hybrid functionals.  In addn., we present results of periodic boundary condition calcns. for both semiconducting and metallic single wall carbon nanotubes.  Using a screened Coulomb potential for Hartree-Fock exchange enables fast and accurate hybrid calcns., even of usually difficult metallic systems.                         cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insights of the anionic redox in P2-Na0.67Ni0.33Mn0.67O2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zuo, Wenhua; Ren, Fucheng; Li, Qinghao; Wu, Xuehang; Fang, Fei; Yu, Xiqian; Li, Hong; Yang, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

                        cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Oxygen redox chemistry without excess alkali-metal ions in Na2/3[Mg0.28Mn0.72]O2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Maitra, Urmimala; House, Robert A.; Somerville, James W.; Tapia-Ruiz, Nuria; Lozano, Juan G.; Guerrini, Niccolo; Hao, Rong; Luo, Kun; Jin, Liyu; Perez-Osorio, Miguel A.; Massel, Felix; Pickup, David M.; Ramos, Silvia; Lu, Xingye; McNally, Daniel E.; Chadwick, Alan V.; Giustino, Feliciano; Schmitt, Thorsten; Duda, Laurent C.; Roberts, Matthew R.; Bruce, Peter G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">288-295</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The search for improved energy-storage materials has revealed Li- and Na-rich intercalation compds. as promising high-capacity cathodes.  They exhibit capacities in excess of what would be expected from alkali-ion removal/reinsertion and charge compensation by transition-metal (TM) ions.  The addnl. capacity is provided through charge compensation by oxygen redox chem. and some oxygen loss.  It has been reported previously that oxygen redox occurs in O 2p orbitals that interact with alkali ions in the TM and alkali-ion layers (i.e., oxygen redox occurs in compds. contg. Li+-O(2p)-Li+ interactions).  Na2/3[Mg0.28Mn0.72]O2 exhibits an excess capacity and here we show that this is caused by oxygen redox, even though Mg2+ resides in the TM layers rather than alkali-metal (AM) ions, which demonstrates that excess AM ions are not required to activate oxygen redox.  We also show that, unlike the alkali-rich compds., Na2/3[Mg0.28Mn0.72]O2 does not lose oxygen.  The extn. of alkali ions from the alkali and TM layers in the alkali-rich compds. results in severely underbonded oxygen, which promotes oxygen loss, whereas Mg2+ remains in Na2/3[Mg0.28Mn0.72]O2, which stabilizes oxygen.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref37" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.6b05111"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Roberts, M. Meanwhile, less CO2 evolution was detected in the coin cell with Na0.67Mn0.65Ni0.2Mg0.15O2 electrode than in that with Na0.67Mn0.65Ni0.2Co0.15O2 electrode in online differential electrochem. mass spectrometry test, suggesting a depressed side reaction between electrode and electrolyte after Mg completely replacing Co.  These comprehensive results provide new viewpoints to explain the improved cycling and rate capabilities from the aspects of crystal structure evolution, coin cell gas evolution and electrolyte/electrode interface cond. during charge/discharge process.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref40" class="refNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit40" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevLett.77.3865"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Perdew, J. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Burke, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ernzerhof, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Generalized Gradient Approximation Made Simple</span>. <i>Phys. Rev. Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1996</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>77</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3865</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1103/PhysRevLett.77.3865</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D77%26publication_year%3D1996%26pages%3D3865%26journal%3DPhys.%2BRev.%2BLett.%26author%3DJ.%2BP.%2BPerdew%26author%3DK.%2BBurke%26author%3DM.%2BErnzerhof%26title%3DGeneralized%2BGradient%2BApproximation%2BMade%2BSimple%26doi%3D10.1103%252FPhysRevLett.77.3865&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c05521&amp;doiOfLink=10.1103%2FPhysRevLett.77.3865&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Generalized gradient approximation made simple</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Perdew, John P.; Burke, Kieron; Ernzerhof, Matthias</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1996</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">77</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3865-3868</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRLTAO</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0031-9007</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Physical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Generalized gradient approxns. (GGA's) for the exchange-correlation energy improve upon the local spin d. (LSD) description of atoms, mols., and solids.  We present a simple derivation of a simple GGA, in which all parameters (other than those in LSD) are fundamental consts.  Only general features of the detailed construction underlying the Perdew-Wang 1991 (PW91) GGA are invoked.  Improvements over PW91 include an accurate description of the linear response of the uniform electron gas, correct behavior under uniform scaling, and a smoother potential.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318817d7d2c54b8-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5884</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c2ee02781j</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5884-5901</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201803436"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Owusu, K. Operando X-ray diffraction anal. revealed the existence of two phase transitions in the voltage window between 1.5-4.3 V.  Ex situ pair distribution function anal. was used to characterize the local structure of the high-voltage phase which is disordered along the c-axis and is derived by the migration of a fraction of iron ions into the interlayer space.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered sodium transition metal oxides with the P2 structure, e.g.  Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2, are regarded as candidates for Na-ion battery cathodes.  On charging, extn. of Na destabilizes the P2 phase (ABBA oxide ion stacking) in which Na+ is in trigonal prismatic coordination, resulting in layer gliding and formation of an O2 phase (ABAC stacking) with octahedral coordination.  However, many related compds. do not exhibit such a simple P2 to O2 transition but rather form a so called "Z"-phase.  Substituting Ni by Fe in Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 is attractive as it reduces cost.  The Fe contg. compds., such as Na2/3[Ni1/6Mn1/2Fe1/3]O2, form a "Z"-phase when charged above 4.1 V vs.  Na+/Na.  By combining ex situ and operando X-ray diffraction with scanning transmission electron microscopy and simulated diffraction patterns, we demonstrate that the "Z"-phase is most accurately described as a continuously changing intergrowth structure which evolves from P2 to O2 through the OP4 structure as an intermediate.  On charging, Na+ removal results in O-type stacking faults within the P2 structure which increase in proportion.  At 50% O-type stacking faults, the ordered OP4 phase forms and on further charging more O-type stacking faults are formed progressing towards a pure O2 structure.  This gives the superficial appearance of a solid soln.  Furthermore, in contrast to some previous studies, we did not detect Fe migration at any state-of-charge using 57Fe-Mossbauer spectroscopy.  It was, however, found that the Fe-substitution serves to disrupt cation ordering in the material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2020.104548"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Dual Carbon Decorated Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>: A High-Energy-Density Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>70</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">104548</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. charging induced phase transition is a common and thermodynamically-driven phenomenon for variety of cathode materials, which couples with chem. and mech. effects leading to performance degrdn.  Phase transition is particularly complex for layered sodium transition metal oxides and its related detrimental effects remain elusive.  Herein, we take P2-type Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 (P2-NNM) as an example to scrutiny the detrimental consequences upon high voltage cycling.  We find that repeated P2-O2 phase transition breaks down cathode primary grains by generating high d. of intragranular cracks, which is qual. proved to be the main cause of performance decay.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are promising for large-scale energy storage due to sodium's low cost and infinite abundance.  The most popular cathodes for sodium-ion batteries, i.e., the layered sodium-contg. oxides, usually exhibit reversible host rearrangement between P-type and O-type stacking upon charge/discharge.  Herein we demonstrate that such host rearrangement is unfavorable and can be suppressed by introducing transition-metal ions into sodium layers.  The electrode with stabilized P3-type stacking delivers superior rate capability, high energy efficiency, and excellent cycling performance.  Owing to the cation-mixing nature, it performs the lowest lattice strain among all reported cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.  Our findings highlight the significance of a stable host for sodium-ion storage and moreover underline the fundamental distinction in material design strategy between lithium- and sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b18226"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">New Insights into the Roles of Mg in Improving the Rate Capability and Cycling Stability of O3-NaMn<sub>0.48</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Fe<sub>0.3</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         rightTab-cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Insights into the Roles of Mg in Improving the Rate Capability and Cycling Stability of O3-NaMn0.48Ni0.2Fe0.3Mg0.02O2 for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Cheng; Gao, Rui; Zheng, Lirong; Hao, Yongmei; Liu, Xiangfeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Elements doping has been used to improve the electrochem. performances of O3-type layered transition metal oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries.  However, their roles and the improvement mechanism have not been clearly understood.  Herein, the effects of Mg substitution for Mn on the structure and electrochem. performances of NaMn0.48Ni0.2Fe0.3Mg0.02O2 have been comprehensively investigated and some new insights into the roles of Mg in improving the rate capability and cycling stability have been presented. (1) The substitution of Mg for Mn enlarges the interlayer spacing, which not only enhances Na+ diffusion and the rate capability but also alleviates the lattice strains induced by Na+ intercalation/deintercalation. (2) The substitution of Mg by Mn also shrinks TM-O bond and TMO2 slabs, which enhances the layered structure stability. (3) The Mg substitution also mitigates the structure distortion or vol. change of the crystal lattices and suppresses the irreversible phase transitions. (4) The substitution of low-valence Mg2+ for Mn3+ reduces Mn3+ and minimizes Jahn-Teller effect, which also further alleviates the irreversible phase transformations and improves the layered structure stability.  This study not only unveils the roles of Mg but also presents some insights into designing the cathode materials with both high rate capability and high cycling stability through the lattice structure regulation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b00614"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, T.-R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Z.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, E.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, X.-D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, B.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cu<sup>2+</sup> Dual-Doped Layer-Tunnel Hybrid Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>1–x</sub>Cu<sub>x</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as a Cathode of Sodium-Ion Battery with Enhanced Structure Stability, Electrochemical Property, and Air Stability</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Layered Mn oxide cathode possesses the advantages of high energy d., low cost and natural abundance while suffering from limited cycling life and poor rate capacity.  To overcome these weaknesses, layer-tunnel hybrid material was developed and served as the cathode of SIB, which integrated high capacity, superior cycle ability, and rate performance.  Here, the doping of Cu was adopted to suppress the Jahn-Teller effect of Mn3+ and to affect relevant structural parameters.  Multifunctions of the Cu2+ doping were studied.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Anionic redox is an effective way to boost the energy d. of layer-structured metal-oxide cathodes for rechargeable batteries.  However, inherent rigid nature of the TMO6 (TM: transition metals) subunits in the layered materials makes it hardly tolerate the inner strains induced by lattice glide, esp. at high voltage.  Herein, P2-Na0.8Mg0.13[Mn0.6Co0.2Mg0.07.box.0.13]O2 (.box.: TM vacancy) is designed that contains vacancies in TM sites, and Mg ions in both TM and sodium sites.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Oxygen-redox of layer-structured metal-oxide cathodes has drawn great attention as an effective approach to break through the bottleneck of their capacity limit.  However, reversible oxygen-redox can only be obtained in the high-voltage region (usually over 3.5 V) in current metal-oxide cathodes.  Here, we realize reversible oxygen-redox in a wide voltage range of 1.5-4.5 V in a P2-layered Na0.7Mg0.2[Fe0.2Mn0.6.box.0.2]O2 cathode material, where intrinsic vacancies are located in transition-metal (TM) sites and Mg-ions are located in Na sites.  Mg-ions in the Na layer serve as "pillars" to stabilize the layered structure during electrochem. cycling, esp. in the high-voltage region.  Intrinsic vacancies in the TM layer create the local configurations of ".box.-O-.box.", "Na-O-.box." and "Mg-O-.box." to trigger oxygen-redox in the whole voltage range of charge-discharge.  Time-resolved techniques demonstrate that the P2 phase is well maintained in a wide potential window range of 1.5-4.5 V even at 10 C.  It is revealed that charge compensation from Mn- and O-ions contributes to the whole voltage range of 1.5-4.5 V, while the redox of Fe-ions only contributes to the high-voltage region of 3.0-4.5 V.  The orphaned electrons in the nonbonding 2p orbitals of O that point toward TM-vacancy sites are responsible for reversible oxygen-redox, and Mg-ions in Na sites suppress oxygen release effectively.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref17" class="rightTabRefNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit17" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.12.083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">McDonald, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type Na0.66Ni0.33-xZnxMn0.67O2 (x = 0, 0.07, 0.14) are prepd. using a conventional solid state method and for the first time developed as promising cathode materials for high-voltage sodium-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, anionic-redox-based materials have shown promising electrochem. performance as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries.  However, one of the limiting factors in the development of oxygen-redox-based electrodes is their low operating voltage.  In this study, the operating voltage of oxygen-redox-based electrodes is raised by incorporating nickel into P2-type Na2/3[Zn0.3Mn0.7]O2 in such a way that the zinc is partially substituted by nickel.  As designed, the resulting P2-type Na2/3[(Ni0.5Zn0.5)0.3Mn0.7]O2 electrode exhibits an av. operating voltage of 3.5 V and retains 95% of its initial capacity after 200 cycles in the voltage range of 2.3-4.6 V at 0.1C (26 mA g-1).  Operando X-ray diffraction anal. reveals the reversible phase transition: P2 to OP4 phase on charge and recovery to the P2 phase on discharge.  Moreover, ex situ X-ray absorption near edge structure and XPS studies reveal that the capacity is generated by the combination of Ni2+/Ni4+ and O2-/O1- redox pairs, which is supported by first-principles calcns.  It is thought that this kind of high voltage redox species combined with oxygen redox could be an interesting approach to further increase energy d. of cathode materials for not only sodium-based rechargeable batteries, but other alkali-ion battery systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref20" class="rightTabRefNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2019.02.042"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Konarov, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jo, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In this study, the effect of Zn doping on the electrochem. properties of P2-Na2/3[Mn1-xZnx]O2 (x = 0.0, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3) is investigated for the first time.         </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type sodium layered oxides NaxMO2 (M = transition metal) are considered as one kind of promising cathode material for sodium-ion batteries because of their known structures, superior electrochem. properties, and their ease of synthesis.  The Ni2+/Ni3+ and Ni3+/Ni4+ redox reactions endow the P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 electrode with a relatively high operating voltage and high specific capacity.  However, the phase transition from P2 to O2 and Na+/vacancy ordering make P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 susceptible to severe voltage and capacity decay.  Herein, we propose to employ the electrochem. active copper(II) as a unique substituent to stabilize the P2 phase, forming Na0.67Ni0.3-xCuxMn0.7O2 (x = 0, 0.1, 0.2 and 0.3).  Our work highlights the importance of Cu(II) in the structural engineering of high performance cathode materials, whose existence can not only stabilize the P2 phase against the notorious phase transition, but also contribute to the rechargeable capacity due to the high potential Cu2+/Cu3+ redox.     <span class="NLM_cas:volume">29</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries have garnered significant research attention in recent years due to the rising demand for large-scale energy storage solns. as well as the high abundance of sodium.  P2-type layered oxide materials have been identified as promising pos. electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.  Previously, P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 was shown to have a high operating voltage and high capacity but suffers from a step-like voltage curve and capacity loss during cycling, potentially due to its P2-O2 transition at high voltages.  One strategy to improve cycling performance has been to dope Ni2+ with other 2+ cations, such as Zn2+ or Mg2+, which improved capacity retention but significantly decreases reversible capacity, since these ions were not electrochem. active.  Since Cu2+ has been shown to be electrochem. active, we replaced Ni2+ with Cu2+, resulting in air-stable Na2/3Ni1/3-xCuxMn2/3O2 (0 ≤ x ≤ 1/3).  Both Ni2+/Ni4+ and Cu2+/Cu3+ participate in the redox reaction during cycling, capacity retention was greatly improved, and phase changes were suppressed during cycling without sacrificing much capacity.  The material retains a P2/OP4 structure even when cycled to high voltages.  The doping strategy is a promising approach for the future development of pos. electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref26" class="rightTabRefNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit26" data-doi="10.1039/D0TA08268F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, J.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, C.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, S.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insights into the Li Incorporation Effect in Ni/Co-Free P2-type Na<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>Cu<sub>0.2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrode materials based on Li-rich layered oxides are of growing interest for high-energy Li-ion battery applications because of their staggering capacities assocd. with the emergence of a novel, reversible anionic process.  However, the fundamental science at work behind this new process needs to be well understood for further optimization.  Here we report on the redox mechanisms in high-capacity Li-rich materials Li2Ru1-xMxO3 and Li2Ir1-xMxO3, by combining X-ray photoemission spectroscopy (XPS) core peaks and valence intensity analyses.  We fully confirm that these materials electrochem. react with Li via cumulative reversible cationic/anionic redox processes, but more importantly we reveal that, depending on the nature of the metal (Ru or Ir), there is a delicate balance between metal and oxygen contributions.  For instance, we show a greater implication of oxide ions for Ir-based electrodes, consistent with the higher covalent character of Ir-O bonds compared to Ru-O bonds.  We equally provide evidence that the oxygen redox process is responsible for the high capacity displayed by the Li-rich NMC Li1.2Ni0.13Co0.13Mn0.54O2 electrodes that are serious contenders for the next generation of Li-ion batteries.  These combined results highlight the benefit of collecting both XPS core and valence spectra for a better understanding of anionic redox mechanisms in Li-rich layered oxides.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref32" class="rightTabRefNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit32" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-017-02291-9"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Assat, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Foix, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delacourt, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iadecola, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dedryvère, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fundamental Interplay between Anionic/Cationic Redox Governing the Kinetics and Thermodynamics of Lithium-Rich Cathodes</span>. <i>Nat.                         rightTab-cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fundamental interplay between anionic/cationic redox governing the kinetics and thermodynamics of lithium-rich cathodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Assat Gaurav; Tarascon Jean-Marie; Assat Gaurav; Foix Dominique; Delacourt Charles; Iadecola Antonella; Dedryvere Remi; Tarascon Jean-Marie; Assat Gaurav; Tarascon Jean-Marie; Foix Dominique; Dedryvere Remi; Delacourt Charles</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Reversible anionic redox has rejuvenated the search for high-capacity lithium-ion battery cathodes.  Real-world success necessitates the holistic mastering of this electrochemistry's kinetics, thermodynamics, and stability.  Here we prove oxygen redox reactivity in the archetypical lithium- and manganese-rich layered cathodes through bulk-sensitive synchrotron-based spectroscopies, and elucidate their complete anionic/cationic charge-compensation mechanism.  Furthermore, via various electroanalytical methods, we answer how the anionic/cationic interplay governs application-wise important issues-namely sluggish kinetics, large hysteresis, and voltage fade-that afflict these promising cathodes despite widespread industrial and academic efforts.  We find that cationic redox is kinetically fast and without hysteresis unlike sluggish anions, which furthermore show different oxidation vs. reduction potentials.  Additionally, more time spent with fully oxidized oxygen promotes voltage fade.  These fundamental insights about anionic redox are indispensable for improving lithium-rich cathodes.  Moreover, our methodology provides guidelines for assessing the merits of existing and future anionic redox-based high-energy cathodes, which are being discovered rapidly.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2021.139421"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rong, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Symmetric Sodium-Ion Battery Based on P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>[Zn<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>1-<i>x</i></sub>]O<sub>2</sub> as Both Positive and Negative Electrode Materials</span>. <i>Electrochim. In large mols. and solids, however, calcg. the exact (Hartree-Fock) exchange is computationally expensive, esp. for systems with metallic characteristics.  In the present work, we develop a new hybrid d. functional based on a screened Coulomb potential for the exchange interaction which circumvents this bottleneck.  The results obtained for structural and thermodn. properties of mols. are comparable in quality to the most widely used hybrid functionals.  In addn., we present results of periodic boundary condition calcns. for both semiconducting and metallic single wall carbon nanotubes. It has been reported previously that oxygen redox occurs in O 2p orbitals that interact with alkali ions in the TM and alkali-ion layers (i.e., oxygen redox occurs in compds. contg. Meanwhile, less CO2 evolution was detected in the coin cell with Na0.67Mn0.65Ni0.2Mg0.15O2 electrode than in that with Na0.67Mn0.65Ni0.2Co0.15O2 electrode in online differential electrochem. mass spectrometry test, suggesting a depressed side reaction between electrode and electrolyte after Mg completely replacing Co.  These comprehensive results provide new viewpoints to explain the improved cycling and rate capabilities from the aspects of crystal structure evolution, coin cell gas evolution and electrolyte/electrode interface cond. during charge/discharge process.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref40" class="rightTabRefNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit40" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevLett.77.3865"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Perdew, J. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Burke, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ernzerhof, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Generalized Gradient Approximation Made Simple</span>. <i>Phys. Rev. Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1996</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>77</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3865</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1103/PhysRevLett.77.3865</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Generalized gradient approximation made simple</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Perdew, John P.; Burke, Kieron; Ernzerhof, Matthias</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1996</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">77</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3865-3868</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRLTAO</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0031-9007</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Physical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Generalized gradient approxns. (GGA's) for the exchange-correlation energy improve upon the local spin d. (LSD) description of atoms, mols., and solids.  We present a simple derivation of a simple GGA, in which all parameters (other than those in LSD) are fundamental consts.  Only general features of the detailed construction underlying the Perdew-Wang 1991 (PW91) GGA are invoked.  Improvements over PW91 include an accurate description of the linear response of the uniform electron gas, correct behavior under uniform scaling, and a smoother potential.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i10">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c05521?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c05521</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images for the synthesized materials, schematic illustration, and corresponding results of crystallographic parameters by Rietveld refinement; capacity retention rates of Na/NMZ, Na/NMZC, and Na/NMC cells; photos of the in situ XRD test equipment; change of the lattice parameter <i>a</i> and unit cell volume of NMZ, NMZC, and NMC; DOS of O 2p, Ni 3d, Mn 3d, and Cu 3d orbitals on Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>Ni<sub>0.125</sub>Zn<sub>0.0625</sub>Cu<sub>0.0625</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with different Na amounts and schematic illustration of the charge compensation mechanism of NMZC; in situ XRD contour maps and the calculated lattice parameter <i>c</i> for the NMC electrode; XPS spectra of pristine and cycled electrodes for NMZ, NMZC, and NMC; O1s spectra of pristine and cycled electrodes for NMZ, NMZC, and NMC; and O<sub>2</sub> evolution of Na/NMZ, Na/NMZC, and Na/NMC cells (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c05521/suppl_file/am2c05521_si_001.pdf">am2c05521_si_001.pdf (1.08 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

            if (document.readyState && !/loaded|complete/.test(document.readyState)){setTimeout(_onload, 10);return}

            scr.type = "text/javascript";
            scr.src = host + "/j/roundtrip.js";
            ((document.getElementsByTagName('head') || [null])[0] ||
                document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0].parentNode).appendChild(scr);
        };
        if (window.addEventListener) {window.addEventListener('load', _onload, false);}
        else {window.attachEvent('onload', _onload)}
    }());

<script nonce="9318817d7d2c54b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="9318817d7d2c54b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {

          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");

      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

                    cookie=status;
